RATING: 7.5
Discworld:Ankh-Morpork is a fun game with plenty of unexpected turns. Has cards, minions, money and buildings, and random events, that do not overpower strategy. Has confrontation, but if you know Discworld, you know it's nothing personal, that's just how Ankh-Morpork is. Your secret winning condition needs to be filled at the beginning of your turn to win. Works well with all of the advertised number of players (2 to 4). Plays always just under an hour.

RATING: 8

Still, the asymmetric victory conditions, and the fun to be had cannot be easily dismissed.

RATING: 6
A very light game of card drawing and making the most of what you have, while aiming for secret goals. The area control aspects are extremely simplistic and the card play is only a small step up from Fluxx. It's very random, with little strategy, and its Lords-of-Waterdeep aesthetics can only serve to mislead players into expecting something more than it really is. Slight, chaotic fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A very nice, easy to learn work replacement game with mechanics that really fit the discworld theme. The game also plays fast (40-60min)and its deduction element is very clear with enough overlap to make guessing which character a person is a challenge. The player aids allows every player to check which character the other player could be easily. Only downside is some players might look at the game and take the game as a pure work replacement game and concentrate on their own objectives only, which can lead to a very quick win for one player with no contest to his goals. 
A good game with good mechanics and theme which trumps Lord of Waterdeep for me personally. Although that is still a game I like too.

RATING: 3
It must help to be a Pratchett fan, familiar with the setting and the many characters on the cards.  If, like me, you're not, Ankh-Morpork offers area control with lashings of take-thatery,  a heavy dose of chaos, and highly tactical play determined by the cards you hold this turn.  The secret objectives seem poorly done; most quickly become obvious and the default winner when the deck runs out seems much the easiest to achieve.

The artwork is quite lovely and the resin models in the deluxe edition that I played are very nice.

Divertido y caótico juego por objetivos asimétricos, con mecánica de mayorías por el control de áreas. Se deben generar estrategias para lograr objetivos individuales asignados de forma aleatoria, de tener la mayoría de peones en cierta cantidad de sectores de una loca ciudad, generar conflictos en todos lados, tener más dinero, que se acaben las cartas sin que nadie gane, entre otros. Eso si, no se tiene tanto control como se espera, de un turno a otro pueden cambiar mucho las cosas, por lo que las estrategias reactivas son mejores que planificaciones a largo plazo.

El motor del juego es el mazo de cartas.

RATING: 9.5
Just love every bit of it, 

- the theme (I love Terry Pratchett's books ... recently bought The Last Hero), 

- the gameplay (every player has a secret personality, there are 7 characters and 5 different strategies), 

- the nice board and components and the outstanding art in the cards, 

- the bluffing (you must hide your true personality behaviour on the board ... at the same time you struggle to get your actual goal),

- the wizards (random events, chaotic outcome of rolling a die ... this is so Ankh-Morporkian!), 

- Ankh-Morpork is a light and fun game, easy to learn, easy to teach, and easy to play (play a card, fill up to five)

RATING: 5
Előre felkészítettek rá, hogy káoszos a játék. Tényleg az:) Tervezni legfeljebb akkor lehet, ha a többiek nagyon nem figyelnek oda. De egyébként elég hamar sejteni lehet, hogy kinek mi a célja, és többnyire sikeresen akadályozhatóak a többiek céljukban. Egyetlen kivétellel: a pakli elfogyása előbb vagy utóbb bekövetkezik, ezt a célt nem lehet megakadályozni! Ez elég nagy hiba, én ezt erősebbnek érzem így elsőre a többinél. Ami fura volt, hogy lement egy 1 órás parti, és se troll, se a démon egyáltalán nem került a játékba. Gondolom ez valami különösen nagy véletlen lehetett.
A játék szép, de se a témája nem áll hozzám közel, se a mechanikájában nem találok olyan különlegeset, ami miatt felkeltette volna a figyelmemet. Wallace helyében inkább megmaradnék az ennél összetettebb játékoknál, ebből valahogy hiányzik a kihívás. Ezzel együtt egy próbát megért, akik a káoszt (vagy a témát) szeretik, és a némi nyelvfüggőséget elfogadnak, azoknak ajánlom.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This absolutely the lightest Wallace's game ever! I am a fan of Discworld novels, so I am an easy target for this game...
It is a very fun, quick and chaotic ride through the lights and shades of the (Disc)world's most interesting city: the turns are very fast, with players playing one card and applying the effects in order. There's a bit of randomness, a bit of take-that, a bit of nastyness... indeed all the things one can expect from a game with this theme!
Of course this ain't Brass or Age of Steam, but it is a very enjoyable gateway game, that conveys the theme through the cards and great artwork.
The only true critic: the game lacks the very things that make the Discworld novels so great... the social critic filtered through british humour!

I would think wallace puts more stock in his name than to put out something this bad.

I've played a 4 player game.

as a game this is faidutti-level chaotic, has noticeable downtime, has no interesting mechanic anywhere, and has the old-school ameritrash approach to adding chrome to the game (add lots of cards with names from the setting, but do not connect the mechanics to the theme at all).

bash-the-leader-until-vimes-wins.

I seriously doubt this is a genuine wallace...

wallace is famous for his distinct 'feel' in elegant game mechanics that make each game an exciting experience to explore for a eurogamer. NOT PRESENT.

wallace is famous for theming his games by ingenious mechanics that manage to represent the subject matter (with god's playground perhaps top of the world in this regard), and not by hoping throwing random effect cards with familiar names/flavor text will evoke theme. NOT PRESENT.

faidutti is famous for a distinct feel to his games that wallace fans can expect not to experience in a wallace. PRESENT :-(

all this would get a disappointed 3-4/10 from me, but what is inexcusable is that the game SCREAMS 'not balanced/tested'. wallace had this amazing ability to put out games that were chvatil-level balanced, every time. the spielbox game perhaps was a first warning something changed and this isn't important to him anymore...

the cost of the building seems totally random compared to their abilities, especially what with the strongest building being given away for almost free to first person in turn order to hold a build buildings card. some of the cards just feel too strong to the point of breaking the game.

the last and most crucial to me problem was the characters - in a 4 player game it felt impossible for anyone to set up any of the conditions for the winning the game, bashing anyone close enough to any of the winning conditions was trivial every time. there was no need to guess who has which character, you easily stopped them all. perhaps for casual gamers this will be a no-issue as people will not notice others getting close to winning, but for wallace fans it's not terribly likely.


chaotic-bashTheLeader-ATLevelTheme games will never be my cup of tea, but this one just didn't seem to work. my final call is: BROKEN (1/10) 


(this is after ONE game, it's quite possible the rating will change with more plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Pro's:

1) The special victory conditions add a great deal to the game. Though I wouldn't say they are all equally balanced, I have yet to see anyone win as Chrysoprase.
2) Aesthetically I love it, the little details on the map of the city are pretty fantastic.
3) IF you are a Terry Pratchett fan, a lot of the card effects really make sense from the way they interact with the game.
4) It is a great intro into area control.
5) There are some potent and fun card combos you can pull off that can be really unexpected and create a totally new turn dynamic.

Con's:

1) The game can be over really quickly if players don't recognize when someone is close to winning. It can be really frustrating when you see someone is about to win and two other players do nothing to try and stop them. I think this comes from the visuals and the mechanics feeling kind of busy and overwhelming to some people.
2) I wish there were more types of victory conditions.
3) The game isn't massive on variety, since you basically stack the deck the same way each time.

Special note:

This was the first modern board game I purchased. For a good year or more it was all we had and all we played. Realistically the rating has probably dropped due to the amount of plays, I am sure I would have rated it higher during that period. I will never get rid of this game though since it is a piece of history and is what brought me into the hobby.

RATING: 3
Soulless and pointless, Ankh-Morpork lacks in both ideas and originality: all you have to do all the time is drawing cards and seeing what you can get out of them, no other skills or strategies are needed. If you’re lucky you can even play a combo with your cards once or twice and that’s all, for it is also your most exciting moment in the whole game. 

Buildings can be taken as the epitome of Ankh-Morpork’s flimsiness: you feel compelled to build houses but houses are useless, for they cost too much, give too little and are prone to be ravaged at the first sneeze around the table. In the end, there’s no substance (and even less testing) behind the notorious Discworld setting.

The game’s art is gorgeous though.

RATING: 7
This is based on one play; the rating could go up. It's an area control game with a "take that" element and asymmetric, hidden victory conditions. That precipitates a lot of conjectural, sometimes half-serious negotiations which can be pleasingly diverting. The cards themselves, particularly if you're a fan of the Discworld novels (as I am) are just funny to look at. Even players who knew nothing of the novels laughed at seeing "Gaspode" pop up and ruin plans again. The cards allow you to make two or three turn mini-strategies which chain throughout the game. Since the gameplay seems to call to mind the word "management" more than a word which equals the whimsy of the cards, I can't really rate it higher than a seven at the moment. Again, this may change. I recommend it especially to fans of the Discworld novels who would appreciate a medium weight three to four player take-that game. There aren't many games that occupy that niche in mu collection so I look to hold onto it and get more plays in the future.

RATING: 4
Provisional rating - might change if I play it more (downwards I suspect).

If you're a fan of the books then I suspect you'll enjoy this much more, doubling my rating ...

So theme is lost on me, as I have no idea who the personalities are, just the overall theme of a fantasy city ... but some stuff I like - (potentially) short, very different hidden objectives. 

And stuff I really don't - lots and lots of randomness/chaos, players can give another player the game if they're not paying attention to *all* the victory conditions, so near the (potential) end it can collapse into AP hell as players worry about setting someone up. Unbalanced cards - if you never draw a building card you can't build ... and these seem quite good for end game vps (converting cash to vps) but also vulnerable to chaotic random events. Cards that let you play cards will extend your turn and seem rather better than the ones that don't. Despite the objectives your turns are very tactical - maximise what you can do now and don't do something to let the player to your left win ...

Overall not a game I'd ever suggest and I'd rather play something else, but I would play again, at least once more (hmm, I'm talking myself into dropping the rating to a 3 after writing this - but I will wait to try it again first).

RATING: 5.8
Juego ambientado en el mundo de Terry Prachet: Mundodisco. En su turno, el jugador se limita a jugar cartas y hacer combos que le pueden permitir putear al prójimo, ganar dinero, activar eventos y/o actuar sobre el tablero, construyendo edificios o introduciendo "secuaces". El tablero está dividido en 12 zonas y en cada una de ellas se evaluan mayorías.

Al principio de la partida se reparten personajes de forma oculta, y según el personaje que te toque, tu objetivo durante el juego será uno u otro. 

LA verdad es que es bastante caótico y no da pie para la planificación...porque cualquier jugador puede desbaratarte los planes. La gracia está en vigilar qué estan haciendo los demás para tratar de averiguar qué personaje tienen. 

No ha sido un gran descubrimiento. Desde mi punto de vista, es prescindible. No soy fan de mundodisco, e imagino que eso influya. Hay mejores opciones en las que invertir algo más de una hora que puede llevar una partida.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
2-4 | (4) | 60 min | 11+ | Martin Wallace

Light-hearted knife-fight in an Ankh-Morpork phone booth.

Easy-to-learn, easy-to-play area control game with hidden roles set in Terry Pratchett's books. Really enjoyable game as long as you don't take it too seriously (be sure lots of backstabbing will happen). While there is luck of the draw (some cards are better than others), there is more skill involved than you might think. The key is building a winning hand and strike at exactly the right time, while trying to stop other players from winning until then.

Fun game, ideal to finish a long session. My rating is closer to an 8 with the right group.

RATING: 7
I wasn't really expecting much out of this title, as I'm completely unfamiliar with the theme and had heard this wasn't a very Wallace'y game, with lots of chaos and randomness.  While it's true that this isn't your typical Wallace game, I still had a good time, however!

Basically, on your turn, you'll play a card or two from your hand that does stuff for you.  The cards do stuff like add your minions to the board, build a building which gains you a special ability, get you money, remove other players' minions from the board, etc.

The goal is to achieve the goal on your character card.  Everyone has a secret character card, so players must watch closely to see if other players are nearing any potential win conditions, as you're never sure what people have!  Some players might be shooting for area majorities, one might be shooting for a bunch of money, one might be shooting for minions in a slew of different areas, etc.  So, there is a lot to watch out for as you try to deduce what goals the other players might have.

Overall, just a fun game that is a bit gamery, but at the same time not to be taken all that seriously.  Even good if you don't care about the theme!

RATING: 7.1
You needn't have any experience with Terry Pratchett to appreciate this game (although the cards might make you curious as to what sort of stories are populated by these ridiculous characters). However, the title, cover and "theme" all give off an impression of weirdness and insanity, making it hard to get non-Pratchett people to the table for this one. 

With that said, it's deceptively great. Let me explain...

On the surface, this seems like a fairly simple game. Play is wacky and chaotic, with ample opportunities to shake angry fists at opponents when they assassinate your little bowler-hatted meeples. I think I would have tired of that after a while. Thankfully, this game has layers. There's a level of intrigue underneath the zaniness. Every player has a hidden goal. The real challenge of this game is to see through the chaos of the gameplay and figure out what your opponents are secretly trying to accomplish.

I've never played Discworld with anyone who didn't enjoy it.

RATING: 7
Treefrog released a Collector’s Edition of the Ankh-Morpork game.

As well as having a different box cover, wooden coins, a larger map (620mm x540mm as opposed to 520mm x 430mm), and a poster with artwork from the game, the Collector’s Edition avoids the use of the number eight (for reasons that should be well known to those versed in Discworld lore). It’s not a big change, but it does mean the production of a special 12 sided die and the separate printing of the cards just to make sure the one with 8 on it has 7a instead (Treefrog will go to any lengths for thematic accuracy!). For those who wish to sleeve their cards you will need the standard European board game size, 59mm x 92mm.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I was pleasantly surprised by this game and almost considering buying it after my first play – which is something I rarely do these days compared to my shopping spree a few years ago (just take a look at my ”previous owned” list)… After my second game, though, I’m not sure it has enough replayability, and the luck is probably a bit too much for what I generally like. However, Discworld is still a pretty good game that I would probably always be willing to play! 

The reasons are that it’s a quick game with some depth which includes quite a bit of the sweet “screw you” factor. Furthermore it has secret goals, and area majority is its main mechanic which is always a plus in my book as it’s my second favorite mechanism (WP being the undisputed number one). What makes the game somewhat unique is the secret goal each person holds throughout the game – these goals ensure that the game can come to an abrupt end! This is a great mechanic as you’re constantly trying to see if your opponents are close to winning. Of course, you might not know this as everybody is trying to disguise their real goals (though it can be quite easy to assess which goals each player owns, but this is generally a problem with this kind of mechanic). The various goals are truly very different (at least some of them), so you need to deploy different tactics in order to win. If you don’t instantly win at the start of your turn, the game will end by everybody tallying their points in the end – unless somebody has the secret character called Vimes, in which case this player wins… Speaking of Vimes, he seems slightly overpowered, at least in a 4p game, so I’m thinking that I might remove him. Of course, if you’re playing with 2 it might also be hard to win with him, so maybe 3p is the sweet spot?

The game worked very well with 3 and 4 players, even though certain secret characters seemed better or worse depending on the player numbers, even though the objectives try to handle this. The screw you factor is present indeed, but everybody knows that this is one of those kinds of games, so people don’t get mad. I think that it’s only because of the light-hearted nature of the game that I don’t get too mad at the excessive randomness in this game. The cards are very imbalanced; some are ridiculously good while others are completely crappy (and no, it’s not good under certain circumstances)! Furthermore, the event deck is very random and might screw you over, but it’s all good as the game takes less than an hour and is indeed enjoyable.
The tension that you have to suffer when you have actually completed your goal, but it needs to be your turn again in order to win, is great; often, the other players will notice that you might win if you have that specific character, so they attack you. It’s a great game of fighting back-and-forth and trying to complete your secret objective!

RATING: 8.5
VERY interesting Wallace.  Don't be fooled by the naysayers who consider this game a foul bucket of randomness and Pratchett kitsch; this game is the real deal.  The rules can be explained in five minutes or less.  Don't bother trying to go throw the intricacies of the player relationships, just play a double header.  

I was initially totally underwhelmed.  But, once you realize that the building aspect of the game provides both information passing as well as a kind of action drafting the deck's roller coaster softens.  The hidden roles effectively turns a Tammany Hall style knife fight into a masquerade ball (knives still included).

Great work Mr. Wallace; I love it.

Game plays well with two, but shines with three or four.

RATING: 8
Terry Pratchett halála után hallani lehetett, hogy a jogokkal baj lesz és nem lesz majd kapható a játék, úgyhogy gyorsan megvettem. Nem bántam meg. Nem árt persze ismerni a Discworld univerzumát, a regényeket, egy csomó kártya plusz értelmet nyer, ha ismered a sztoriját a rajta szereplő személynek, de anélkül is nagyon élvezetes a játék.

Ez volt talán az első olyan játékom amiben minden játékos  teljesen más célért küzd. Valaki akkor nyer, ha minél több területet ural, a másik akkor, ha kifogynak a kártyahúzó paklik, a harmadik ha felhalmoz egy bizonyos pénzösszeget, stb. Az is a játék része, hogy figyelj a másik vajon mire játszik, és aktívan akadályozd abban, hogy elérje a célját. 

A mechanika egyszerű: megjátszol egy kártyát, lejátszod a rajta lévő akciókat, majd visszahúzod a kezed a maxig a húzópakliból, ha kevesebb maradt, mint amennyi az alap kézméret. Akció az is lehet, hogy újabb kártyát kijátszol - ezeket lehet chain-elni, szóval van, hogy valaki csak pakolgatja lefelé a kártyákat a kezéből és egy lánckombinációval nyeri meg a végefelé így a játékot. Akcióval építhetsz tornyokat területekre, amikkel kapsz bónuszkártyát, ez belevisz plusz egy taktikai lehetőséget a játékmenetbe. 

Jó érzés belemerülni a Korongvilág univerzumába, a könyvek olvasása után a tematika is működik, de olyan is jól érezte magát játék közben, akinek semmit nem mondott Széltoló meg a Poggyász neve.

RATING: 7

RATING: 5
This game is mostly a 'feast of recognition' for those who read through the approximately 20 first Discworld novels. You can immediately recognise if someone has read the novels or not, by the way: ask them to pronounce the name of the very, [i]very[/i] scary Mr. Teatime. In my mind the characters look different, but it's fun to see them drawn, played and manipulated just the same.

Onto the game: there is much talk of it being a simple luckfest, but I don't buy that, at least not in its entirety. Martin Wallace is a better designer than that. The different ending conditions mean that you cannot play your own game: you have to be continuously on the lookout for these conditions to apply, and seek out means to avoid them from arising in the first place. And with the fiendish condition of Vimes in there (who will win if the the draw deck runs out) you cannot play entirely leisurely either. Your cards, randomly drawn as they are, nearly always allow you to do something useful, be it something constructive for your own position, or something destructive to prevent another condition from appearing on the board. It's not a complex mechanic by any means, and yes, there is likely to be something in the criticism that it makes play too tactical for 'serious gaming'... But it certainly doesn't make things [i]easy[/i]. I liked the game I played: it's not a great [i]game[/i], but I think this is a fairly rare example of a game which is perfect for once in every while.

RATING: 5
[2012-09-18 Rating: n.a. -> 5]

Discworld: Ankh-Morpork is an easy to grasp game. Each turn you play a card, do what it says and draw back to your hand limit. Rinse and repeat this procedure until one of the players reaches his secret win condition and ends the game. 
Since this is a card game, there is also luck involved, but to a certain degree it can be dealt with by using the districts' special abilities. What is much harder to control is the player induced chaos. To quote a friend of mine: "You can't really plan your next turn since it's quite certain that a lot of the board will have changed until it's your turn again". 
Strangely, this doesn't bother me as much as I would have expected. What keeps Discworld:Ankh-Morpork from getting a better rating is the fact that I simply don't have much fun while playing the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players represent one of the notable persons of the city of Ankh-Morpork, trying to achieve their hidden victory condition.  Super simple game of placing and moving pieces around the twelve areas of Pratchett's Ankh-Morpork.  A player's turn is simply playing one or more cards, and following the icons that allow them to place minions, build buildings, take cash, and other assorted actions.   These should be driving you towards achieving your victory condition, which you have to hold until the beginning of your next turn, not an easy thing!  The victory conditions are nicely varied, although some appear more difficult to achieve than others - mainly because they are so obvious (such as "amass 50 cash", and "place 8 trouble markers").  Still, very light, breezy, random, but importantly ... fun.   This is no stodgy over-engineered Wallace game, it's more like a chaotic Faidutti game that actually works!  Well done.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
Overall this game is a lot of stuff from other games thrown into a blender. The way some cards chain is pure Dominion; the area control is classic El Grande, and the hidden agendas go way back to Illunati. Though the combination does feel unique.

My gripes are limited to the lack of any sense of control. Everyone is looking around beating back those who get near a victory condition. Your hand is really random, and with a finite amount of special actions you may never see some crucial ones come into your hand. 

We found it a very managable game to teach & learn in that the cards do all of the explaining.

RATING: 8.5
Gyönyörű játék, pláne, h nekem a collector edition van, könnyedebb, mint egy Wallace játék, pedig ugye az. Azaz nem egy tipikus Wallace, már olyan szempontból, h nem a gamers game tagja.
Csak szórakoztatni akar, de azt jól teszi.
A Collector's editionben nyilván, ahogy a hangulathoz, és a helyhez illik, az a bizonyos 7 és 9 közti szám nem szerepel benne :-)
A kártyák gyönyörűek: tényleg a Korongvilág szereplői, olyanok, amilyennek az ember -vagy legalábbis én- elképzeltem a könyvek , vagy mint a 3 film alapján.
És nem véletlen módon vannak a szerepek (győzelmi célok) sem az egyes karakterekhez rendelve: a világ alapján: Lord Venitari pl nem véletlen sok helyen akar jelen lenni a győzelemhez, vagy Vimes parancsnok célja nem véletlen az, h senki ne tudjon semmi rossz fát tenni a tűzre, azaz lemenjen a játék anélkül, h bárki teljesítené a célját.
Szóval jók a szereplők, jók a kártyák: a Korongvilág nem csak egy ruha rajta, hanem beleillesztették a világba a játékot.
Ugyanakkor a játék alapvetően arról szól, és ezért aztán van interakció, h ugyan nyerni akarok, de folyamatosan megakadályozzam a többieket abban, h ők meg nyerjenek.
Egyetlen igazán nagy hibája van: durván nyelvfüggő: a kártyák egy jelentős részén szöveg is, ami egy plusz akció lehetőség. Vannak persze egyszerűbb szövegek is, de vannak picit hosszabbak is, nem mondom, h pár játék után a nyelvet nem tudok nem jegyzik meg - pláne ha ismerik a korongvilágot, de ettől még komoly nyelvfüggőség.
A játék idő is változatos: ha vki ügyesen eltereli a figyelmet akkor akár gyorsan is nyerhet, de ha jól észlelik a többiek h ki mit akar, és jól akadályozzák, akkor jóval hosszabb is lehet. nekünk 1x kb 40 perc, egyszer 1,5 óra volt - mindkét esetben 4 fővel.
Viszont végig érdekes marad, izgalmas, vidám, mókás.
Amit tudni kell még, h a "rajzolók" közt az eredeti Korongvilág grafikusai, művészei is jelen vannak, pl: Paul Kidby, vagy a a művész team vezetője: Bernard Pearson. Szóval nem véletlen, h a lapok úgy néz ki ahogy, és h "élethűek"

RATING: 10
For fans of discworld they will love to see some of their favorite characters from the novels make appearances in this VERY fun game that has it all. It has strategy and tactics that are fun on every level. All players are dealt a random character. Each character has a goal to reach in order for that player to win. There are several different goals.  Not only do you have to watch the other players to figure out who they are and what their goal is to try and stop them, you have to also use your hand to reach your goal all the while making sure they don't know who YOU are.  You do not have to be a fan of Terry Pratchett or read a single discworld book to appreciate this game, but if you read the series like I do, every card is like an Easter egg. WOW that guy was briefly mentioned in Going Postal!  etc etc. I bought this game because I love the books...I love this game because it is balanced, fun with great art and a bit of that Terry Pratchett humor.

RATING: 6
This is an area control game where everyone has a secret game ending goal. The area control is driven by card play. The cards themselves have various icons which allow you to do various actions. One of the icons is to play another card which allows you to chain actions. The crux of the game is trying to determine what other player's goals are while trying to obfuscate what your own are. There is a heavy dose of randomness and take-that, but it plays quickly, looks gorgeous and is pretty fun. I like it and would be happy to play it from-time-to-time.

RATING: 3
Yet another flaky Wallace design. A family game ruined by the usual insane multiple game end/win conditions that perhaps only worked once in Liberté. You are at the mercy of cards drawn and there is little to mitigate bad luck in draws.

On top of that, the one win condition based on filling the board with trouble markers seems problematic. It depends on all players working to keep the board clear of trouble, and if a player gets the city that allows regular trouble removal then they have to spend money to act as board cop, preventing that win condition for everyone else.

Finally, it's terrible with two players. It could possibly work if the trouble marker and the deck elimination wins are removed. The trouble player is pretty much insurmountable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
9/2011 

Martin Wallace is one of my favorite game designers, and I am delighted that he is publishing in new genres.  This is a light game with a few, simple rules.  I think players who love the books upon which this game is based, or games like Killer Bunnies, will enjoy this game.  You play a card, do what it says, and draw back up. Your win conditions are those of your secret character.  That is it. It is card driven, so there is a lot of luck involved, but there is some strategy, deduction, and bluffing, too.  Most of the fun comes from slamming others.  

I ordered deluxe version from Treefrog site which has wonderful wooden components, and the red dragon on the box. During pre-order, I fell in love with that magnificent red dragon, so was really surprised when all the cards were cartooney with garish colors, which I find unpleasant.

I don't enjoy this type of game very much--I like a more strategic game.  Of course, it didn't help that on the first game I played, that for over half the game I was reduced to 3, in-hand cards and never had a coin in front of me that wasn't taken before I could spend it.  My opponents died laughing, and it was pretty funny, but it also made a boring game.  It took and hour and I was unable to play anything for 40 of those minutes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.2
 You are one of the characters from Terry Pratchett's Discworld book series vying for control over the city of Ankh-Morpork.  There are seven lords to choose from.  Three have the same goal, while four have quite different ones.  You must complete your goal before your opponent finished theirs, and the only way to do this is by playing cards from a community deck.  
  This is a pretty fun game, though luck is very important.  It's fun to put down your little wooden agents, (I like to pretend they are thugs in a gang war) and have them cause havoc in Ankh-Morpork.  Much of the fun comes from the card's names and pictures, each represents a different character from the books.  What makes the cards even better is the effects usually agree with the character on the card.  My personal favorite is White Face, who is a mime who will follow you around unless you give him money.  
  The game is not perfect however.  As every action you take is determined by either the cards you play, or the buildings you own, luck is a pretty big factor in this game.  The cards are not of equal value.  Most cards allow you to do one or two unconnected things, while some are really powerful and allow you to do a combo from just playing it by itself.  Skill will help you make the most while you wait for these super cards.  Only when you get them do you feel like you can really make a move and piece together a combo.
  Despite the reliance on luck I think this is a pretty good way to pass an afternoon.  Do you have to know who most of these characters are?  Yeah, to get a kick out of playing cards you kind of do. This limits who you want to play it with a little.  People who don't know the characters will play it, but they will get board pretty quickly.
  Who do I think will get the most out of this game?  I think you need to have three things going for you.  You need to: 1) enjoy board games enough to spend $40 on one, 2)have read a few Discworld books and have enjoyed them, 3) don't really want a competitive game, then you really should get the game.  The last two will points probably go hand in hand, but I thought the distinction should be clear.  With that said, I don't think this game will sell very well.  Then again Martin Wallace is a pretty famous guy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Being an avid Pratchett fan, I had high hopes for [b][i]Discworld: Ankh-Morpork[/i][/b]. Unfortunately, this is not my ideal Discworld game because it feels very chaotic as you play. I suppose that fits the theme, but it doesn't translate super well to a game in my opinion. (On a side note, I'm pretty certain my ideal Discworld game would expand upon the game between Fate and the Lady that is played in The Colour of Magic and Interesting Times). I like it most as a 2-player game where the game's chaos is mitigated.

That said, Discworld: Ankh-Morpork is not a bad game. It's a quick-playing hectic encounter filled with memorable Discworld characters. It's essentially an area control game, and the hidden objectives add an interesting twist as to exactly what areas are best for you to control. Every game is fun, it's just that individual games are not as memorable as I would like.

RATING: 4
The game has simple rules and enjoyable gameplay, but it is very chaotic and luck-driven, with plenty of take-that actions. The theme is a big plus for me, as I enjoy the "Discworld" books, but the cards should have some flavor text, because I suspect not even the most fanatic reader will know and remember all the characters.

The cards vary wildly in power, and although most seem useful, there is even one that does absolutely nothing. The secret goals (victory conditions) are even worse, completely unbalanced in difficulty. If everyone is paying attention, one seems impossible and a few are very hard to attain, while there is little or nothing that opponents can do to mess with two others. This eventually killed the game for me. I had fun playing and gave it a fair shake, but it became increasingly difficult to accept the flaws, once the novelty wore off.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I have only read one book of the source material, but I imagine that this game is a must-have for fans of the series. While each card has a different illustration and name, some have an identical function, so the appeal will come more from recognition of characters than real integration of theme.

Primarily an area control game, most of the actions are done through cards. The simple "play a card, then refill your hand" style works very well. With five cards in hand, many providing the ability to play multiple cards, it's generally pretty easy to accomplish something towards your goal on your turn. The real challenge is that you also need to worry about stopping other people from doing the same.

This is a beautifully illustrated game with excellent production quality (despite one unfortunately very confusing typo). It's very much a gateway game, though the it can be hard at first to prevent others from winning. This is probably the biggest drawback in my book. It is very difficult to watch out for several other people potentially achieving one of five different goals. That said, getting a different goal each game provides some fun variety. 

This seems like a game that doesn't really provide enough depth or variety to want to play it for years to come. Even so, it's enjoyable enough that I'd always be willing to play. That could change after a lot of plays though.

RATING: 3
(05/2012) Comment based on one play.

I was very disappointed with this Martin Wallace design, as it was rather simplistic, chaotic, and seemingly random. You have a hand of cards, each with symbols that allow you take specific types of actions. If you're lucky, you may be able to string together multiple actions and gain good presence on the board. As for the board, we were left scratching our heads about what we were trying to accomplish on the board. Is it area-control? No, but you do want to get enough money to buy a building in a specific area to give you possibly recurring benefits.

Our game ended rather quickly at about 15-20 minutes with one player achieving his secret objective easily. The general feeling was "meh".

I don't know who Pratchett is, and the group was not familiar with his work, so the theme wasn't compelling and felt pasted on. There were some trademark Wallace mechanisms scattered here and there, but overall didn't feel like a Wallace game. My knee-jerk rating is a '3', with little patience to try this again.

RATING: 5
2012-03-02

Initial Rating: 5.0

This is the worst Wallace game I have played so far, it is too light for a Wallace game, most people here on BGG say the game is very thematic which would be a huge plus for me if it were true, it is thematic (I guess)  if you have read the books, which I have not, maybe if I have I could have appreciated the game better, but for me this game doesn’t tell a story. At first glance it appeared a good game with interesting decisions, but as it has so much luck, so much chaos, your plan can and will come crashing around you. The victor is determined mostly by luck which I didn’t expect from Wallace and that is my main complaint with the game.

The game board and the art is beautiful, I  give you that, however in the Collector`s Edition there is a huge typo in the player aid, the character Vetinri (I believe) says he wins when there are between 7 and none trouble markers, it took a while to shed light in this and it is a minor concern, however as it was the collector´s edition I feel mistakes like this should have been caught.

I love secret role games but playing this I didn’t have much fun.

RATING: 7.5
On the surface, this looks like a pretty basic area control game. It definitely has some hidden depths, though. The asymmetrical goals make for some very interesting in game deduction. The cards are fun and varied (and if you're a fan of the Discworld series, as my wife is, you will probably get very excited about all the familiar faces). The only sort-of negatives I see are that game's randomness can definitely screw a player over, and the fact that win conditions must be present at the start of your turn, which basically makes leader bashing a big part of the game. It may be possible to disguise who you are enough to sneak a win, though, we'll see. Good game, though, I'm glad I picked it up.

RATING: 6
Having now read the first 7 Discworld novels (I started reading because I bought this game on whim feeling guilty at a game store), I still don't get many of the references. You must either need to read them all, or just the books that were most recent when the game came out. Anyway, the game is very light. It has awesome art on the cards, but the game board is a muddy abomination. The actual gameplay has a tendency to feel imbalanced in favor of four of the seven secret roles (everyone assumes that you have on of the three that are the same). 

I never felt like my card playing mattered as much as I wanted it too. The presence of those cards that allow you to play other cards make the game luckier than I would like. Also, most events seemed like they very rarely would have a significant effect on the gameplay. Some events were nearly gamebreaking. In the game we played, the person who won achieved half of their trouble-marker goal when the event deck turned up the troll card. It made all the rest of the work we did feel severely pointless. 

I still want to give it another go, but I'm not feeling terribly impressed.

RATING: 7.5
Played a demo copy at Kubla Con over Memorial Day.  Really enjoyed the creative, area-control mechanics.  There were some nice thematic elements to the game, although I'm not familiar with the novels.  With each player having a different, secret winning condition, there's a meta-game that lays over the mechanics as you try to infer what your opponents' winning conditions are and try to disguise your own.  Given that your winning condition must be met at the *start* of your turn, disguising it becomes paramount.  In my case, I had to try to over-achieve my winning condition so that my opponents couldn't reduce my position during their turns.  Didn't work :(

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is likely the least strategic and is the easiest Wallace board game to pick up and play but I find it very entertaining to play as long as the people get into the spirit of things and don't mind nasty play.  This is NOT your typical, gamers' game that Wallace for which he is known.  

LIKE:

* Cards have symbols at the top indicating which actions you can take in that specific order.  Outside of the event card icon, you can choose to skip an action(s).  
* The different instant win conditions each person has creates fairly aggressive game play in which people are constantly hammering on others in the game.  If you don't like mean games, stay FAR away from this one.
* Hey, the "7a" on the die instead of the "8" is a nice, thematic touch!  :-)
* This game is very simple to teach and play.  
* The special extra actions one can take from building in a region is a nice touch as well.

DISLIKE:

* First games may drag slightly as people figure out the cards and how to chain some together effectively.  

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Probably the lightest Wallace game to date.  Very fast to play, 30-60 minute games, easy to teach, and everyone has fun.  Can't wait for my Deluxe Edition to arrive.

RATING: 5
It's a real chaos which does reflect the books and universe. However, despite much efforts this can also create serious imbalances.

Whilst the cards are nice and all different, this also means you will be spending an awful lot of time with you, yourself, and your hand, and when you play them, no one really cares or pays attention at the artwork, and only looks at the result, because everyone wants the game to move on...

To sum up, a good concept that however suffers from serious game flaws (imbalances, time, etc.) and that do not exploit the universe enough, leaving me with an unpleasant aftertaste.

RATING: 7
An area control, area majority game where each player has a different goal.  You try to cover up your goal as best you can throughout the game and not let other players discover it.  You are playing cards that create trouble, provide money or build buildings.  If at the start of your turn, your winning condition is met, you win.  

A relatively light game which I really enjoyed.

RATING: 6.5
It feels as though being familiar with the Discworld novels would add considerable value to the game. The cards are all unique and each features different characters from the series, but unfortunately, you don't get much of a sense of what the Discworld theme is all about from this game alone. That's a pity, because there's a bit of chaos in this game that, presumably, would be more excusable if one understood how it was justified by the theme.

That having been said, this is by no means a bad game. It's a solid and accessible area-control game with sound mechanics, and it is definitely a game for those who are already in the hobby (or just getting into the hobby). It's just that those who know and love the source material will get considerably more enjoyment out of this.

RATING: 8
*Collector's Edition:

This game is a lot meatier than I anticipated--so much going on. The massive card deck should offer great replay value. After two plays, I feel that I've only scratched the surface. Color me surprised. I expected this to be a simple hidden character role game with a little area control sprinkled in. yet, unexpectedly, this game has a lot more to offer. Does that make it better? Not necessarily. There's certainly more to chew on, but it also forces you to compare this to other light-medium weight games. In that category, Discworld loses points. In short, I really like this game, and I hope my rating will improve with time.

Update 12/3/14:

I played last night with Erin, after almost a year without playing. I forgot how fun this game is. We definitely played it wrong our first 2 games (we didn't do the setup in the three initial districts). Because of the rules misread, we were running through the whole deck without meeting character-ending conditions. After playing the right way, we never made it to the brown deck. At 30 minutes, this game is awesome. At 90 minutes, it's still really solid, but a little long for what it is. Nevertheless, after experiencing the shorter game twice in a row, I have to increase my rating of the game. Part of the reason I played it was to see if I wanted to keep it. I'm not getting rid of this one for a while!

RATING: 7
I am a fan of the Discworld series, so the theme greatly appeals to me.

What I like:
- Humorous and fun if you play it lightly
- Random events can be pretty hilarious because of the destruction it leaves behind
- Different phases of the deck brings more tension and chaos to the second half of the game
- Hidden roles provide opportunities for role bluffing, and also provide some variation
- I think the 3 player game is best because there's less downtime and it feels like it's harder to stop other players compared to a 4 player game.  There seems to be a good balance between games ending where players meet their win conditions and when the deck runs out.

What I don't like:
- It is too chaotic and random if you try to play it competitively
- The Lords roles are a little boring
- It seems too easy for Vimes to win in a 4-player game, and too hard in a 2-player game

RATING: 8
A fascinating frenzy of fantastic features come together to create the chaotic Discworld: Ankh-Morpork.  In this game players get a secret identity, each of which has their own agenda that must be completed to win.

The game has a heavy area control component with lots of cards that can be played to allow for different things to happen on the board.  Each card in your hand will have one or more symbols along the top.  You must play the symbols in order from left to right, but you don't have to play the ones you don't want to!  The exception to this is the random event, which must be played.  The random events do mix things up quite a bit in an already pretty random game.

The cards have very funny art and names which will entertain you as you play.  So, if you don't mind chaos and silliness in your game you really should give this one a try.  I love this game.  The components are nice, and trying to figure out what the other players' goals are, and throwing a wrench in their plans is fun.  I highly recommend.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Played a proto-type at Cancon 2011.

Pratchett fans of the Discworld universe will love this. Even if you're not a fan of Discworld (I'm not), the game is still quite engaging, very much card driven, but without that need to "churn through cards". All cards will are able to do something everytime your turn comes up & the balance seems pretty good for each card as well. The secret victory conditions for each player keep it tense, needing to know what the conditions are & then trying to ensure that only yours is met, it makes for a good game.

Looking forward to the finished article.

RATING: 6
I'm a fan of the late Sir Terry Pratchett's Discworld, but I'd say this game works regardless of theme (though it does fit, and the cards are amusing even if only for the artwork and familiar names).

It's an interesting blend of what is a rather simple area control game and a simple card-play mechanism that drives it (play a card, do the action(s), draw back to five). It works because it is not the sort of game where you try to build incremental advantage, maximizing your points per turn. Instead it's a game of bluffing and illusions, feinting to the left while going right. Because every player has a hidden goal, which tells them the the only way that they can win the game. However, if you just mindlessly work towards that goal you find that it's rather easy for the other players to stop your progress cold if they know what you are after - so you have to bluff and feint your way to victory (sculpting the perfect hand, forcing the perfect sequence where you win because your opponents are stretched too thin and can't stop it because they saw it too late).

Quite random, with the cards, and even moreso with the chaos events, but that is alright by me, I still feel clever when I win - or see where I went wrong when someone else does. It rarely feels unfair in that someone is just handed their win due to luck of the draw. The one complaint about this game, that the Vimes role is broken, well I'll just repeat what the defenders of the role say, stop doing his job for him. If everyone is mindlessly blundering towards their own goal, just don't be the one to waste effort on stopping them, just try to race instead. It is not a game of perfect information, people will make mistakes and prioritize the wrong threats and that's when you have to be in position to strike.

A good game!

RATING: 6
Wallace goes light this time with a thematic majorities game where everybody has secret winning conditions.  I enjoy it although it's quite chaotic at times in both how the cards play out and why somebody chooses to attack another. You can try to guess what winning conditions your opponents have but at the end we're usually bashing anyone that's close to possibly winning. From what I've seen the end (when a victory condition is achieved) usually comes with a groan from everybody else.  

For me one of the biggest disappointments is that it's pretty difficult to bluff because the winning conditions require you to be very strong in one aspect of the game -- something that usually takes quite awhile to build up to. If you mess around trying to fool your opponents you'll probably lose.

RATING: 6
Ankh-Morpork is a high screw, high chaos, low complexity area control game with a Discworld series theme.

There's very little you can do to maintain any control in this game, as any movment towards a possible victory condition is almost always lost beneath a sea of screw actions between each of your turns from the other players!

Amusingly all players must still pay close attention at all times to each characters actions though, as it IS possible to win within a couple of turns if left unmolested!

The easiest way to win is to draw the Captain Vimes winning condition, which is simply to stop anyone else from winning until the action deck runs dry (which will usually be the outcome with a competative group).

Fans of the book series will enjoy spotting all the character references as an additional extra, but its not necessary to know the series to enjoy the game.

Ultimately a light experience which should not be taken too seriously, Ankh-Morpork is a good gateway game for non-gamers.

RATING: 7.5
Wallace goes light and uses the world of Terry Pratchett to create a great game. This game is all about the 'take that' factor as the players try to scrabble their way to the top of Ankh-Morpork whilst trying to keep their identity and win condition hidden for as long as possible. 

The game is card driven and a series of icons keep the game flowing very smoothly. Players can enjoy the game without being Discworld fans but it does add a little spice and excitement when the players know the characters in question.

Fans of heavy Wallace should not look here...this is a light family/gateway game for sure.

Half the fun is in accusing other players of being a certain character and trying to throw off people's suspicions as to who you are. As such this game is all about the group playing it.

RATING: 6
Ankh-Morpork is wonderful for fans of Terry Pratchett. Fans of Martin Wallace' deeper games may find it lacks complexity, however, and should probably try before they buy. 

The board shows a map of Ankh-Morpork; players have a hand of cards, and in turn they play a card for its effect then redraw. The cards feature great art and strongly integrate the theme. Some cards let you place extra workers, play extra cards, upset your opponents... Gameplay is all about achieving a set of secret victory conditions and announcing success at the start of a new turn. Play is mainly tactical, with only a little opportunity to plan ahead. There is a lot of "swat the leader".

Ankh-Morpork is light, tactical, needs to be played fast and should be a pleasant 45 minute experience.

RATING: 5
Easy and nice game which mimics a town of Discworld setting. Probably knowing the setting can raise the my rating.
The game is essentially a card game, with a map on which you drop minions to control areas. It works smoothly and is fun. It has the very good idea of semi-secret individual objectives, which avoid the all-agains-one effect. However, 3 problems:
1) card drawing is really really random. Drawing many cards with the symbol "play another" makes your hand too pawerful
2) events are really chaotic, what you put on the board can easily disappear
3) houses are WORTHLESS. Unless you have the house which protects other houses, owning any house is a waste of money

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is fun when you're winning, experiencing Martin Wallace blend Euro into a thematic ride. But when you're the victim of horrible and out-of-proportion randomly hit losses which knock you out the game, not so much fun. Probably very thematic, but not satisfying. The back theme is that the ruler of Ankh-Morpork has disappeared and each player is one of the factions trying to take over the city in their own style. As such, each player has a secret mission, which may be controlling x of the 18 districts on the board, getting 50 money, getting x trouble markers out onto the board, getting minions into x districts, or simply making sure no one completes their secret mission before the deck runs out. So an essential part of the game is a wary watchfulness over what everyone does. Your own turn is to play cards out of your hands to affect what's going on around the board. Cards combine text actions and icons, which may be to build buildings, earn / steal money, place / move minions, trigger god-like disaster events from the event deck, play another card from your hand and so on. Each card's usefulness and actions are thematically linked to characters / places in the book(s) so readers should enjoy it. For gamers, it's a combination of building up your presence on the board with some hitty on other players. It comes in on the hour which is ok. The decisions on which card or cards to play, how and who to impact, without giving away your secret mission, and occasionally ganging up to hinder other players from what you think they might be doing, is the essence of the game. Given the forced end-game time clock of the draw pile running out (which may be someone's secret mission in itself, they're not all in each game), there can't afford to be too much ganging up - you've also got to focus on getting your own goal done in time. If no one gets their secret mission, there's a VP adding up thing at the end based on resources deployed. Turns are fast as the cards are easy to play out. There's not a huge amount of tension because all of what you can do is dictated by the 5 cards you have. And that's about it. Play for the theme.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Yes, it's very chaotic, not just because of how quickly the board state changes, but also because of the hundreds of different cards with varying usefulnesses.  On the flip side, it's a somewhat relaxing activity to try to string together cards in your hand to try to advance your goals as much as possible, without giving an opponent's goal too much of a boost.  I imagine this plays fairly differently with 3 (where you can probably figure out what everyone is going for) and with 4 (where it's much harder).  If you like fast-playing optimization games where you constantly have a barrage of options thrown at you and you don't get too attached to your board position, you might enjoy this game a lot.  Bonus points if you're a fan of the property.

(1 play)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a very fast, very punchy game, where each player is given a secret goal, and a hand of 5 cards. On a player's turn they can play up to 2 cards, and do the actions on the card in the order shown at the top of the card. Then they draw back up to 5. If at the start of someone's turn, they have achieved their secret goal, then they win. The magic of the game is trying to take actions that would make someone believe you have a different goal than you actually have, so that when they punch you, your real goal doesn't get set back. The rules are very simple, but there's still a lot to think about, as you always need to be making educated guesses as to what other people are going for and how to stop them.

Something about the game that bothered me was the degree to which one has to pay attention to the board state. There are character roles who win if at the start of their turn if they control four areas, and that's a very easy goal to achieve if one or more players stop paying attention for a second. This can get tough when your secret goal is something like, collect a conspicuous amount of money, or have the game deck of action cards run out. It means game sessions are either amazing, or done disappointingly soon.  For example, in our fourth play, I was money guy, and players could kinda see I was stockpiling cash, so all the punish cash cards were being thrown my way. I was only a few turns in, and starting to build some steam, and then a player who needed to control four regions declared victory; game over. For my time, I would rather be playing A Study In Emerald.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:nostar: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::nostar::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
The game is gorgeous to look at and the mechanics are tight enough, but drawing your winning condition randomly paired with the constant random drawing of cards makes for a game heavily reliant on luck.  Once player's deduce the personality cards present in the game, there's basically a random back and forth between players until one of two things happen- 1. No one draws a card that game stop the leader. 2. The deck runs out and THAT personality wins, or you add up points.

I don't think the personality cards are balanced appropriately. There's not enough ways to mess with the money personality and if you to advance towards your own goal you really can't help but help the deck run out of cards for another personality to win.

Overall: I like the basic mechanics of the game however it loses it's charm quickly as you start to notice the tedious back and forth nature of the game. A few small tweaks and this could be a real winner, but for now I'm just not terribly impressed.

RATING: 7
I like the books and I also like the cards showing the characters of the books. First I thought there is much luck in the game because of the drawing of the action cards. But after some games I must say that I am able to control the game more than I first thought. For example the "Mr. Whiteface" is an evil card when played against you because it restricts the number of hand cards from 5 to 4. But you can counter this effect if you pay 5$. So make sure you always have 5$ ready to pay and you're save. You can also take over cards to the next round, so you can plan and don't depend too much on card drawing when you want to do a certain action, like for example creating a building. 

Chaining (playing several cards during your turn) offers the possibility for nice combos.

Warning: it can be very frustrating when the other players destroy or take over your buildings because they noticed you're about to fulfill your characters victory condition. Players should be capable of digging this. Good bluffing about your character (and therefore victory condition) helps ;-)

Update: In our games Mumm tends to win.

RATING: 6
13-08-2015. Rated after 1 play. Ankh Morpork is based on a theme I don't care about, those books from one guy called Terry Pratchet..

IN the game, you have the city of AM. You have a hand of 5 cards that you try to play to obtain one of your goals. These goals are hidden, but are diverse enough that you can spot it during the game.
3 of the goals are based on controling territories on the board, some you need money, of trolls, or devils on the board. 

AM has a lot of take-that, screwage and interaction, it might actually be my favorite wallace, albeit his 'worst' game i played.

RATING: 8
Very enjoyable game. One of the first "proper" board games I played, and I've used it several times since to get people into board gaming. The theme is whimsical and humourous, so despite people not knowing the books the players adapt a similar persona to throw jabs and insults at each other.

Still played as a medium-light game that takes about 30-45min, usually end up playing several times, as people get into it.

It has it's problems and pure strategy is hard to come by as everybody kicks the about-to-win player in the shins - but as a not-so-serious (small) party game it is a good opener. It is very good at making you feel like you could win if you could just do this one thing, but then everything conspires against you ensuing in hilarity for all. A game of this is usually followed by a lot of excited descriptions of how many times people were about to win and what they tried to do to not let people know their true goal.

RATING: 8
First Impressions: I don't really know why I preordered this except that I am pretty much a Wallace fanboy, and did enjoy the first few Discworld novels as a teenager. I am wary that my opinion may change, but I have certainly enjoyed the game so far after 4 2player games against Mrs Schkff. The board is a lot more important to the game than the one in London (to take another Wallace card game themed on a chaotic and malodorous metropolis as an example), the randomness is fun, and balanced by the fact that the cards only come in a small number of different types. Also, the trouble mechanic is classic Wallace - at first seems odd, then proves simple, thematic and gamey. The system wouldn't accept a 7a rating, and 9 seems a bit high just yet (might get there though) so for now this is a safe 7. 
[Edit after first 4 player game]: People who think that Vimes always wins are bufoons. Sorry, but this is true.
[Edit after reading Guards! Guards! for the first time] I can't get over how true to the spirit of Pratchett's writing this game is. Love it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Reddit suggests: http://www.reddit.com/r/AskReddit/comments/lz567/what_are_some_board_games_actually_worth_buying/c2wszfk
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Readers of the books are going to enjoy the artwork as characters are brought to 2D life, the city is laid out before them and certain character actions loosely match what they'd expect from said characters. Those unfamiliar with the books are just going to find themselves stumbling through a chaotic, near random piece placing game.

Let's face it: Figuring out who's who isn't that tricky here. There's only so much bluffing you can do on the way to accumulating $50, or spreading minions out on the board as you try to build building in the two areas that allow you to buy further minions, or, or, or... So, you're at the mercy of the cards and the wild fluctuations they have in power (useful to your particular needs). It's good to be Vimes, but if you've read the books you know that already. Okay, the game is chaotic, but I can still enjoy it as a fan of the books, right? Well, yes and no. While I enjoy the flavour and the attempt at creating a simple 30-40 minute game, for me the mechanics are too dumbed down for their own good. There's a lack of meaningful decisions where I can say I played a good move. No satisfaction after a win. Frustration at being picked on and having three turns unraveled in one.

I reckon this is a much better two or three player game than it is with four. There's more scope for bluffing and the ganging up on the leader element is removed or reduced. I'd play it happily with two and less so with three and no much at all with four. If your spouse is a Discworld fan then this short and easy 2-3 player game might be for you.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is as simple as play a card and do whatever it says. It feels like a game of achieving majorities, but it really isn't, cause every player have a different objective and not all of them will have to control areas to win. Simple, fast, beautiful artwork, nice bits and a great story behind.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game is just unbalanced in a bad way. Everyone draws cards from a common deck, each card has a number of symbols which are the actions that card does (and some have text that's executed). One symbol lets you play another card. This is a problem because at the end of your turn you draw your hand back up to 5. So if you have a hand of cards that don't let you play another your turn is play, draw, go. This would be fine but while you're doing play, draw, go, your opponent is chaining off 3 cards per turn since playing more cards means drawing more cards and having more impact on the board due to nothing but luck.

The second problem is the secret win conditions. Each player has 1 (of I believe 9) win conditions to fulfill, these are all spelled out clearly on a very well done player aid. If you meet your victory condition at the start of your turn, you win. This is a problem because as a player you have to make sure the next player won't have any of them met when your turn is finished. This isn't too hard, really, if you're paying attention, and also the other players will point it out. The problem arises in that it's really too easy to prevent this from happening, and one person's win condition is to run the deck out of cards. You can't prevent that in any interactive way. So if everyone makes sure to not let the others win, and someone has this win condition, they will probably just win. You could play without that win condition, but the 'tiebreaker' win conditions for running the deck out are pretty unsatisfying.

I get that this is a light game, it's just not a terribly well thought out game. I'd guess it's target audience are fans of the novel, who if are not gamers would probably love it (because it's a very nice production and the game "works").
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Fairly simple game considering it's Wallace. Players have a hand of cards and play off one card each turn and draw a card. Actions are played onto a board from Terry Pratchett's Discworld series.

There are hidden objectives and cards help achieve it. Some objectives include area control, winning if NO other players achieve their objectives, earning a certain amount of money.

Martin binned the prototype after the convention.

A játékosok lapokból játszanak és térképen megpróbálnak a céljuknak megfelelő területet foglalni és házakat építeni. Egymás akadályozása nagyon fontos, de emiatt a királycsináló rész is nagyon jelen van a játékban. Mindenkire nem lehet figyelni. A kártyaelfogyós célt pedig akadályozni se lehet. Az, hogy milyen lapot kap a játékos a kezébe mindent eldönt. De nem ezek voltak a fő gondjaim a játékkal, hanem, hogy túl egyértelmű. Sokat nem kell rajta gondolkodni, teljesen egyértelmű melyik kártyát kell kijátszani. Egyes célok még minimális gondolkodást sem igényelnek a játék közben. Tehát túl egyszerű és túl szerencsefüggő. Ehhez még jön a nagy nyelvfüggőség. Aki a játék témáját ismeri, olvasta annak biztos nagy élmény lehet játszani, mert nagyon szépek a kártyák, aranyosak a szövegek. Nekem csalódás volt, de könnyedsége miatt azért nem utasítanék el később se partit vele.

Mr Wallace has created another winner. I haven't read the books, by the way, and I love the game, so don't worry at all if you haven't. Way. Easier.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2 half-plays

[b]--- Game Summary ---[/b]
The board shows Ankh-Morpork, divided into 12 numbered regions. Each region shows a $ value and is associated with a specific building (cards set nearby). There's a deck of cards (sets A and B; A on top): shuffle and deal each player 5? cards. Some minions are placed on the board (randomly?). Players each get a secret random card, which defines their win-condition (e.g., have minions in x regions; collect $50 in cash and assets; control x regions; etc.). If your win condition is present at the start of your turn, you win!

On your turn, play a card and use the icons shown at the top (left to right; most optional, some required). These may give you money, allow you to place a minion or buy a building, draw a card, play another card, etc. Then use the text on the card, which has a variety of effects. Any time you place or move one minion with another, add a Trouble Token (if one isn't already present). These prevent players from buying the building in that region, and are related to one of the win conditions (have x regions with Trouble tokens). If you use a card to move another minion away, the trouble token in the original location (if any) goes with it. Your turn is all about the cards you get to play, and the actions they give you (or if you're unlucky, the single card you get to play). When you build buildings, you get more abilities you can use each turn.

[b]--- Comments ---[/b]
I guess if you're a fan of the book series, or at least somewhat familiar with the characters and story, the game may be more entertaining. For me, it was mostly a way-too-chaotic experience. I felt totally at the mercy of the cards I happened to draw; and I rarely drew the cards that would let me play more than one per turn! There are also a lot of cards that allow you to target other players (moving or removing their minions, messing with their hands or their buildings, etc.); that gets to be pretty annoying, too! 

I suppose I wouldn't mind trying this again (a full game, one sitting!) with a fan of the game; but it doesn't at all seem like my kind of game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
2-4, 60m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is a sort of area control game with hand management and hidden roles.  Each player starts the game with a hidden role which details their victory condition which is usually based on the board state.  All players know the possible characters and victory conditions in the game so while trying to accomplish their own goal can also try to figure out what roles other players have and prevent them from accomplishing their own.  When a player's turn starts with their condition met they win the game.

The gameplay itself is very simple.  On your turn you play a card from your hand.  The card you play has a list of symbols across the top that affect the board in different ways (such as placing your pieces out or removing others) and many also have their own unique text ability.  Some also let you play other cards so you're not necessarily left to one card play per turn.  I actually quite like this.  The game has a good amount of variety on the 100-something cards while also being very simple to teach and also plays pretty quickly.  Probably an hour or less with the full complement of 4 players.

My absolute favorite part of the game is the artwork though.  I now want to read all the Discworld books because the pictures on the cards are beautiful and delightfully wacky.  The very first card played in my first game was titled "Librarian" and had a picture of an orangutan eating a banana and reading a book on it.  How do you not just want to know the story behind that?

I do have some issues with the game though that keep me from loving it.  Mostly with the imbalance and lack of variety among the hidden roles.  There are 7 possible roles and they are not all created equal.  With the need for having your victory condition met on the start of your turn there is a lot of opportunity for other players to mess with your plans when you've actually set yourself up before it gets back to you.  One of the role's victory condition is for the deck of cards to run out without another player winning.  This seems like it is one of the easier ones to accomplish because you simply need to play defense the whole game (which the other players are most likely going to be doing as well) until you get to the end.

Also, there are 7 roles and 3 of them are the same; control a certain number of areas (based on number of players).  This makes it both a little less interesting and a little easier to figure out your opponents roles when close to half of them have the same victory condition.

Besides my issues with the roles though this is a great, accessible game that teaches and plays quickly with a lot of interaction and fun images.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I played a prototype April, 2011.  The game may change - I certainly hope so.  At this point it appeals only to Pratchett fans in that it evokes the mood of the Discworld books very well.  Unfortunately for gamers who are not Pratchett fans, that mood includes a heck of a lot of chaos.  It's impossible to plan anything.  So if these rules don't change by the time they go to the printer, and you're not a Discworld fan, this game won't please you.  Pratchett fans will be pleased with the atmosphere and laugh heartily throughout the game.  Laughter is good.

RATING: 6
I wanted to like this game.  I still might, but the game I played was so short, I had a hard time deciding if I even liked it our not.  I have never read any Discworld so I have no idea about the theme or how well it matches; but it did seem to be an interesting setting. 

Ripped: Each player has a secret personality with specific victory conditions, which means that you're not sure exactly what the other players need to do in order to win.
The action takes place on a map of Ankh-Morpork, with players trying to place minions and buildings through card play. Each of the 132 cards is unique, and says Wallace, "The cards bring the game to life as they include most of the famous characters that have appeared in the various books. The rules are relatively simple: Play a card and do what it says. Most cards have more than one action on them, and you can choose to do some or all of these actions. Some cards also allow you to play a second card, so you can chain actions."

 I like the idea of 'trouble' and using markers to determine where the 'trouble' is and being able to manipulate the trouble to your advantage.  The cards seem to be a little too random., but since you have a hand of them, you can mitigate the luck through planning.   The game ended shockingly early as one of the players hidden victory conditions was attained very early.   The excuse was that once you've played the game and learn the victory conditions of the other characters, you will be able to protect against that and it shouldn't end so quickly.   It seems to be too easy of an excuse to me.   I need to play it again in order to get a better feel for it.  I am sure my rating will change after even one play that lasts more than 15 minutes.

RATING: 5
This is the "Smash Up" of area control games: it's surprisingly light-weight, frustratingly chaotic, and you can't even guess what the play area is going to look like by the time it gets back to your turn.  I played the Collector's Edition (or was it the Deluxe Edition??), so all the components were quite nice, with a large, spacious board, tactilely pleasing wooden tokens, and nice cards with cute artwork.  That being said, I've never read a Pratchett book, so I'm sure that a lot of the thematic elements flew straight over my noggin.  The truly simple game play was a plus for a game this chaotic, but I don't think that 4 players is an ideal player count due to the frequency of board-altering events that make long-term strategy a futile endeavor.  In fact, it is my belief that with 4 players it is impossible for any of the lords whose victory condition involves controlling at least 4 regions for an entire turn to win as long as the character whose win condition is to have the deck run out is in play, due to the fact that all players are keeping a suspicious eye on anyone who controls too many regions and all have an opportunity to do something about it before it gets back to that player's turn again.  That was a rather long sentence and I'm not sure that it made much sense (but that's also how I might describe Discworld: Ankh-Morpork).  It might be a decent game to play with kids if it weren't for the fact that much of the game involves tearing down what other players are trying to build up.  Oh well, I guess I need less pork in my life, not more.  Game design: 3/5  Personal enjoyment: 2/5  Total: 5/10

RATING: 7
