=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Hmmm. Played twice with full complement of four; the second game was quite different than the first, though Vimes won both.

There's a lot of luck to the game and the cards restrict your options a lot, but there are goals to achieve and a variety of decisions to make about how to achieve those goals -- as with many luck-heavy games, the question is how much your decisions [i]matter[/i].

The main reasons to buy are the gorgeous, thematically appropriate components (particularly in the collector's edition, where the natural numbers go 7, [url=http://wiki.lspace.org/wiki/7a]7a[/url], 9). For Pratchett fans, it's a feast: every card is named for a character or location from the Discworld, beautifully illustrated, and has a (vaguely) appropriate set of actions.

The biggest downsides for me are that I'm not sure how it will hold up once the novelty wears off, it has the potential to run long, and it may be too complex for non-gamers who aren't that interested.

I'm losing some enthusiasm after seeing three Vimes wins in three games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Area majority game with hidden goals set in Terry Pratchett's Discworld universe. At the start of the game each player is given a secret character card that determines what they need to do to win e.g. gain control of a certain number of areas, acquire a certain amount of money, playing through the game until the deck of action cards is exhausted etc. Gameplay is driven through the playing of cards which allow certain actions to be taken, such as placing agents onto areas of the board, assassinating opponents' agents, triggering events etc, and these can be combined for greater effect. 'D:A-M' is a fairly chaotic affair but it incorporates its theme well and should be of some interest to fans of Pratchett's books (one of which I am not particularly). Designer Martin Wallace always brings something new to his games, and in this case the simplicity of the cards' actions works really well, but overall there isn't really enough substance here for 'D:A-M' to rank alongside many of his other designs and it all feels a little too light.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
TESTA
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.25
A card-based strategy board game for 2-4 players set in the (here beautifully illustrated) comic fantasy city of Ankh-Morpork. Simple and quite fast (45-90 mins) to play, but very strategically deep and varied due to the different (and secret) win condition each player is dealt at the beginning of the game. For example, your condition may be you only win by spreading your minions widely; the other goals are amassing money, or controlling a proportion of the city,* or simply stalling or causing trouble. So there is an element of social deduction, but unlike Werewolf style games you do not win by guessing your opponents' identities.**

So far so good, but it gets better! It works just as well with 2 players as with 4. Further, each of the over a hundred central cards are different, often very silly and fun with their abilities, which adds lots of variety. Moreover, on every play those of us who are Discworld fans continue to enjoy the many references, but those we've played with who know nothing about it do not suffer from a feeling of missing out, and can understand the game perfectly well. Ultimately, play is very fluid with all the mechanics working so well, it would be easy not to notice all the strategy going on underneath the beautiful surface. But Ankh-Morpork does indeed have the best balance of strategic complexity to ease of any game I'm aware of. The main downside is how luck-based the ending can get if a lot of 'random event' cards are played. But with the overall quality, it's fantastic that Discworld creator Terry Pratchett lived to see his work celebrated by this high class product.

* Given that you can own property that in areas you don't control, it seems strange that you can't have a secret identity who wins through building property rather than controlling areas, so there's space to improve the game there.

** Although I think you could make a house rule where you could gamble something on guessing correctly.

RATING: 7

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is an area control game.  It is rather simple to play and much simpler than it looks.  Games don't last too long.  A 3-player game takes less than an hour to finish.

Each player randomly draws a personality card at the beginning.  This card determines the win condition for the player.  Most of the win conditions are based on achieving certain board positions.  One is based on getting enough money.  One is to make sure no one wins by the time the game ends. 

During a turn, you play a card which lists what actions they may take during the turn.  Some cards allow you to play more cards.  Actions include, but not limited to placing a minion, building a building, killing a minion in an area with a trouble marker, and removing a trouble marker.  Some cards have special effects listed in the text of the card.  Some actions costs money or possibly allow you or other players to gain or lose money.

You can build buildings which will give you some ability you can activate each turn.  You will need to pay for the cost of the building.

Some cards cause random events to happen which could help or hinder you or your opponents.  The random events add an interesting element in the game.  They also appear on more powerful cards you play.

As the win conditions vary between players, part of the strategy is to determine what the win conditions of other players are.  Sometimes, you need to play a card to prevent another player from winning instead of advancing towards your win condition.

RATING: 7.5
This is an interesting litte game. My sister owns it and there it stays. But there are many times when I wish I owned it so I could play it more. I'm a huge Wallace fan, and here he manages to accomplish a simple, thematic, light-weight game that still feels like Wallace. What I mean by "Wallace" is that even though the game is on the lighter end of the spectrum, the decisions still feel relatively heavy. Every decision you make is important, and there are a plethora of good/interesting decisions at every turn. I like the implementation of hidden identities in the game. It works well. The more players get to know the characters in the deck, the more fun this game becomes trying to figure out who everybody is, and eventually becoming a game that involves a bit of bluffing and curveballing. RATING: 7.2
Played Prototype with 4 players - Entertaining lightish card driven game.  Good use of theme.  Basics of the game are each player has a hidden objective which if met (generally for the start of their turn) allows them to win the game.  This is also a bit of a downside as the latter stages of the game turn into everyone trying to ensure the player after them does not win, or just trashing possible winning positions.  This is not the only game that does this, however I did find it quite frustrating (possibly as I had given away my objective too early in the game).  Would be more than happy to play again though.

RATING: 7
A great family game, perhaps the best there is. The card imbalances, deduction pleasures, and terribly fun blocking work really well in a non-gamer environment. When the parents get out-psyched & trounced by the kids, well, it's special. Very funny cards & artwork, beautifully rendered. 

I also would love to play this with gamers (I haven't), but I think it's be a fun romp & have a nice player-driven imbalance.

RATING: 7.5
Played once. First impressions.  As with anything related to Terry Pratchet this game is wacky, and that's a good thing.  The art is pretty kool and the game is well designed and appealing.  I like the randomness in that you don't know what your win conditions are going to be before you start the game.  Having different win conditions for the different personalities also makes the game more intriguing and, in a way, chaotic.  The game is all strategy with a little bit of luck depending on the cards you draw and dice rolls.  With only two players it can get a little cut-throat.  Immediately after the first game there was a call for a rematch!  Unfortunately time did not allow for one.  I will definitely be playing this again.

Two more plays and raising my rating slightly.  Enjoying the variety of "Personality" win conditions.  Still only played two player, definitely want to get more players in on this.

RATING: 8
Worker placement where the meeples cut each other's throat.  Going into this game you will need to realize that planning is limited by random card draws from a common deck.  Luckily the small amount of dice rolling adds very little to the overall randomness.  There are many crazy moments (typically between 7 and 9) so even though this is a gotcha game it is family friendly as well. The game design is extremely refined for the level of depth, like a well made animation version of a classic tail.  In terms of fluff the skin fits well with the mechanics.

RATING: N/A
This game is very hard for me to pin down.  It's chaotic, and almost entirely tactical (rather than strategic) as a result, but as long as everyone is paying attention to the possible win conditions, your decisions are still meaningful.

I'm not sure I would appreciate it as much, or even have given it a second chance, if the theme wasn't applied so well to the elements.  I wouldn't say "integrated", but applied is definitely accurate, and as a fan of the books, that probably lets me overlook the level of randomness inherent in the cards a little more than I would otherwise.  

The game also teaches SUPER-quickly, as it's play 1 card, do what the symbols say in order (reference the player aids for what the symbols do) and draw up to your 5 card hand limit.

RATING: 7.5
Fast-paced, easy to play area control game.  The hidden roles with independent paths to victory are really what make the game.  Otherwise it's a pretty simple game of just playing cards and taking the actions with some random chaos thrown in at the end of the game.

It's great for not over-staying it's welcome.  There's a momentarily slow start as people start claiming their initial territories.  Things quickly heat up as competition rises, and then things get really chaotic as the events start popping up and then it's over.

I wish the player count went a bit higher and it'd see more play.  I so often have 5 players that when I do have 3 or 4, there are a lot of great games to chose from.

RATING: 6
I'm not sure how this game would be for folks who aren't into Discworld.  There's definitely an interesting set of mechanisms here (and some that remind me a lot of Martin's style, which is no surprise, really), and no dearth of randomness and chaos in the game, but understanding the references and characters from the series is a large part of enjoying the game.  It has interrupt cards (possibly my least favorite mechanism of them all), and event cards which have the potential to destroy whatever progress you've made up to that point (my [i]second[/i]-least favorite mechanism), but it all fits in with the theme rather perfectly.  It's not one to own, by any means, but it sure is a lot of fun to play.

1 play: 4 players

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very chaotic, but lots of room for strategic maneuvering also.  If you don't like take-that card play or random events smashing up your hard work, steer clear!

Despite the compounded randomness of the cards and events, there are really a lot of interesting decisions to be made each turn--enough that I don't feel like the game is lost in the chaos.  There's hand management in the card play, area control in the minions, and a small amount of economy building.  The game is mostly tactical--doing what you can with the cards you have--but I often found myself planning a few turns in advance to set myself up for a building that fit into a larger strategy.

I'm not familiar with Discworld universe, but the the theme is really fun anyway and the art is fantastic.

Even though there's no direct fighting and no distinct factions, the card-directed area control and the player- and game-generated chaos feels a bit like Chaos in the Old World to me.  That game is definitely tighter and deeper, but this one feels a little like a Chaos in the Old World game spinning out of control.  I think it's fun.

RATING: 2
Theme: none.
The cards have names from the discworld universe. The game board claims to be Ankh-Morpork (but doesn't fit the description from the books). You could rename the cards to any setting - ancient, sci-fi, whatever - and wouldn't need to change anything, because there is nothing related to a specific theme. Lots of cards with generic actions - gain some gold, remove a cube, gain a buy, draw a card, gain an extra card action. In fact, it looks like card design was greatly inspired by Dominion.

Gameplay:
Area control at its worst. Lot's of 'bash the leader'. Totally at the mercy of the cards - one player could only play 1 weak card per turn for most of the game, while another one was able to constantly play 2+ more interesting cards.

Balance:
One faction wins when the deck runs out. Unless one player gets very lucky with his cards, he wins, since you are usually able to prevent the other victory conditions from happening. 

Personal experience:
At one point, i stopped to play against other players, i just wanted the game to end. Unfortunately, it didn't. When the deck run out, it turned out that the one faction wth the auto-win wasn't in the game. We didn't bother to count points.

Caveat:
I know one player who totally likes this game, for the pure chaos. Most players were glad when the game finally ewas over.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Lord Vetinari has vanished and the opportunistic factions of Ankh-Morpork are trying to increase influence of the city in his absence.  As special characters of these factions we each have separate winning conditions. You have to first remove trouble markers from an area. Otherwise, it has all the basic trimmings; play a card, activate a card, move a minion, control an area, build something, roll some dice, draw a card. Event cards will cause area disturbances like trolls or demons or riots oh my.  You can string together big moves. The trick is to figure out the other players to interfere with their winning objectives. Light game with decent decision points. Nice board of the city and great job capturing the history and feel of Ankh-Morpork. As a fan of Pratchett, this one likely makes its way to the collection.

RATING: 7
I did a bit of playtesting for this. A decent family-orientated game with good thematic elements and moderate randomness. I love the hidden victory conditions but their variety means that in the first game you may not be paying attention to how other players can win. With subsequent games, this issue goes away (or you can carefully study all the win conditions in advance).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very simple mechanics for a Martin Wallace game. Fairly tight, though there may be a problem with the winning conditions for Vimes if everyone playing is overfamiliar with the game - i.e. players can spot a winning condition fairly easily, leading to stalement that results in the game ending when the deck is exhausted. If Vimes is in play then that player wins. Obviously, the way around this is to become very good at bluffing and playing a strategy outside of your characters winning conditions to put people off the trail. The danger though, is that you might help another player out. 

The cards a beautiful though, and the random events suitably chaotic. Good fun...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A 2-4 player area control game. Players take turns playing cards from their hand to take the actions on those cards, trying to complete a secret objective and preventing others from doing the same.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A solid one from Martin Wallace. I give it a 8, because too much in this game depends on luck, meaning the disasters that happen randomly, and the uncertainty of said events. That means that even if one player has made great strategical progress it can all go to hell and you can do nothing about it.  

Otherwise I love the theme (being a Pratchett fan) and I think that the game mechanics are a pretty solid base for a competitive area control game, featuring satisfying mechanisms to eliminate your opponent's minions and keep the control over an area.

RATING: 7
Love Discworld! And this game is pretty awesome too! What I love about this game is that it requires ZERO knowledge of the books to play. It's got great game play mechanics, it's fun, and can be cut throat at times. If you have read the books it does of course make you chuckle at the cards a little and you get the inside jokes but again it's not necessary as this game really stands on it's own.

RATING: 8.5
Unquestionably good game. Nothing wrong with its design. Fast, fun, tactical without too much of a learning curve. Appeals to new players and experienced gamers alike. No need to know the world, but fans will adore it for that all the more. From a person who actively likes to modify their games to try and establish an ideal sense of balance, there was nothing I could find at flaw with this game beyond it may lack story (which I personally like). But mechanically it is incredibly sound and functional.

Cannot emphasize enough. A must have for any gamer collection.

RATING: 7.5
Surprisingly good game. Knowing nothing of Pratchetts world most of the characters (and therefore humour) was lost on me, but the game is solid enough that that doesn't matter. There's elements of bluffing and misdirection as you try to convince your opponents your secret objective is something other than it actually is, whilst you try to manoeuvre your position to actually win.
High levels of random is partly a shame but also adds to the chaotic, frenetic nature. I really like this one.
